<li><i><a href="/wiki/Incertae_sedis" title="Incertae sedis">Incertae sedis</a></i>
<ul><li>†<i><a href="/wiki/Camptophyllia" title="Camptophyllia">Camptophyllia</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></li>
<li>†<i><a href="/wiki/Parioscorpio" title="Parioscorpio">Parioscorpio</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
<li>†<i><a href="/wiki/Wingertshellicus" title="Wingertshellicus">Wingertshellicus</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Marrellomorpha" title="Marrellomorpha">Marrellomorpha</a></li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Thylacocephala" title="Thylacocephala">Thylacocephala</a></li></ul></li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th colspan="2" style="text-align: center; background-color: rgb(235,235,210)"><a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">Diversity</a>
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align: center"><a href="#Diversity">around 1,170,000 species</a>.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th colspan="2" style="text-align: center; background-color: rgb(235,235,210)"><a href="/wiki/Synonym_(taxonomy)" title="Synonym (taxonomy)">Synonyms</a>
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align: left">
<p>Condylipoda <small>Latreille, 1802</small>
</p>
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p><b>Arthropods</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span><span title="/θ/: 'th' in 'thigh'">θ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>/</a></span></span>, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> <i> </i>ἄρθρον<i> (arthron)</i> 'joint', and <i> </i>πούς<i> (pous)</i> 'foot' (gen. ποδός)) are <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrate</a> <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a> having an <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">exoskeleton</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Segmentation_(biology)" title="Segmentation (biology)">segmented</a> body, and paired jointed <a href="/wiki/Appendage" title="Appendage">appendages</a>. Arthropods form the <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phylum</a> <b>Arthropoda</b>. They are distinguished by their jointed limbs and <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_cuticle" title="Arthropod cuticle">cuticle</a> made of <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a>, often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mineralization_(biology)" title="Mineralization (biology)">mineralised</a> with <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a>. The arthropod <a href="/wiki/Body_plan" title="Body plan">body plan</a> consists of segments, each with a pair of appendages. Arthropods are bilaterally symmetrical and their body possesses an <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">external skeleton</a>. In order to keep growing, they must go through stages of <a href="/wiki/Moulting" title="Moulting">moulting</a>, a process by which they shed their exoskeleton to reveal a new one. Some species have wings. They are an extremely diverse group, with up to 10 million species.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haemocoel" title="Haemocoel">haemocoel</a>, an arthropod's internal cavity, through which its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haemolymph" title="Haemolymph">haemolymph</a> – analogue of <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a> – circulates, accommodates its interior <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_(anatomy)" title="Organ (anatomy)">organs</a>; it has an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_circulatory_system" title="Open circulatory system">open circulatory system</a>. Like their exteriors, the internal organs of arthropods are generally built of repeated segments. Their <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a> is "ladder-like", with paired <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location#Dorsal_and_ventral" title="Anatomical terms of location">ventral</a> <a href="/wiki/Ventral_nerve_cord" title="Ventral nerve cord">nerve cords</a> running through all segments and forming paired <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganglia" title="Ganglia">ganglia</a> in each segment. Their heads are formed by fusion of varying numbers of segments, and their <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brains</a> are formed by fusion of the ganglia of these segments and encircle the <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">respiratory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Excretion" title="Excretion">excretory</a> systems of arthropods vary, depending as much on their environment as on the <a href="/wiki/Subphylum" title="Subphylum">subphylum</a> to which they belong.
</p><p>Arthropods use combinations of <a href="/wiki/Compound_eye" title="Compound eye">compound eyes</a> and pigment-pit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocelli" title="Ocelli">ocelli</a> for vision. In most species, the ocelli can only detect the direction from which light is coming, and <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_eye" title="Arthropod eye">the compound eyes</a> are the main source of information, but the main eyes of <a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">spiders</a> are ocelli that can form images and, in a few cases, can swivel to track prey. Arthropods also have a wide range of chemical and mechanical sensors, mostly based on modifications of the many bristles known as <a href="/wiki/Seta" title="Seta">setae</a> that project through their cuticles. Similarly, their reproduction and development are varied; all terrestrial species use <a href="/wiki/Internal_fertilization" title="Internal fertilization">internal fertilization</a>, but this is sometimes by indirect transfer of the sperm via an appendage or the ground, rather than by direct injection. Aquatic species use either internal or <a href="/wiki/External_fertilization" title="External fertilization">external fertilization</a>. Almost all arthropods lay eggs, but many species give birth to live young after the eggs have hatched inside the mother, and a few are genuinely <a href="/wiki/Viviparity" title="Viviparity">viviparous</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphids</a>. Arthropod hatchlings vary from miniature adults to grubs and <a href="/wiki/Caterpillar" title="Caterpillar">caterpillars</a> that lack jointed limbs and eventually undergo a total <a href="/wiki/Metamorphosis" title="Metamorphosis">metamorphosis</a> to produce the adult form. The level of maternal care for hatchlings varies from nonexistent to the prolonged care provided by <a href="/wiki/Eusociality#In_insects" title="Eusociality">social insects</a>.
</p><p>The evolutionary ancestry of arthropods dates back to the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">Cambrian</a> period. The group is generally regarded as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophyletic" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a>, and many analyses support the placement of arthropods with <a href="/wiki/Cycloneuralia" title="Cycloneuralia">cycloneuralians</a> (or their constituent clades) in a superphylum <a href="/wiki/Ecdysozoa" title="Ecdysozoa">Ecdysozoa</a>. Overall, however, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basal_(evolution)" title="Basal (evolution)">basal</a> relationships of animals are not yet well resolved. Likewise, the relationships between various arthropod groups are still actively debated. Today, Arthropods contribute to the human food supply both directly as food, and more importantly, indirectly as <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollinators</a> of crops. Some species are known to spread severe disease to humans, <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>, and crops.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word <i>arthropod</i> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%84%CF%81%CE%B8%CF%81%CE%BF%CE%BD#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:ἄρθρον">ἄρθρον</a></span> <i>árthron</i>, "<a href="/wiki/Joint" title="Joint">joint</a>", and <span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CF%80%CE%BF%CF%8D%CF%82#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:πούς">πούς</a></span> <i>pous</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genitive" title="Genitive">gen.</a> <i>podos (ποδός)</i>), i.e. "foot" or "<a href="/wiki/Leg" title="Leg">leg</a>", which together mean "jointed leg".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OnlineEtDict_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-OnlineEtDict-5">[5]</a></sup> The designation "Arthropoda" was coined in 1848 by the German physiologist and zoologist <a href="/wiki/Karl_Theodor_Ernst_von_Siebold" title="Karl Theodor Ernst von Siebold">Karl Theodor Ernst von Siebold</a> (1804–1885).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>In common parlance, terrestrial arthropods are often called <b>bugs</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[Note 1]</a></sup>  The term is also occasionally extended to colloquial names for freshwater or marine crustaceans (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balmain_bug" title="Balmain bug">Balmain bug</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moreton_Bay_bug" title="Moreton Bay bug">Moreton Bay bug</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mudbug" title="Mudbug">mudbug</a>) and used by physicians and bacteriologists for disease-causing germs (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superbug" title="Superbug">superbugs</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walbauer1_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walbauer1-10">[10]</a></sup> but entomologists reserve this term for a narrow category of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/True_bugs" title="True bugs">true bugs</a>", insects of the order <a href="/wiki/Hemiptera" title="Hemiptera">Hemiptera</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walbauer1_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Walbauer1-10">[10]</a></sup> (which does not include ants, bees, beetles, butterflies or moths).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Arthropods are <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> with <a href="/wiki/Segmentation_(biology)" title="Segmentation (biology)">segmented</a> bodies and jointed limbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">exoskeleton</a> or <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_cuticle" title="Arthropod cuticle">cuticles</a> consists of <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a>, a polymer of <a href="/wiki/Glucosamine" title="Glucosamine">glucosamine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> The cuticle of many crustaceans, <a href="/wiki/Oribatida" title="Oribatida">beetle mites</a>, and millipedes (except for <a href="/wiki/Polyxenida" title="Polyxenida">bristly millipedes</a>) is also <a href="/wiki/Biomineralization" title="Biomineralization">biomineralized</a> with <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a>. Calcification of the endosternite, an internal structure used for muscle attachments, also occur in some <a href="/wiki/Opiliones" title="Opiliones">opiliones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diversity">Diversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Diversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Estimates of the number of arthropod species vary between 1,170,000 and 5 to 10 million and account for over 80 percent of all known living animal species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> The number of species remains difficult to determine. This is due to the census modeling assumptions projected onto other regions in order to scale up from counts at specific locations applied to the whole world. A study in 1992 estimated that there were 500,000 species of animals and plants in Costa Rica alone, of which 365,000 were arthropods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson1994CoevolutionaryProcess_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson1994CoevolutionaryProcess-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>They are important members of marine, freshwater, land and air <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>, and are one of only two major animal groups that have adapted to life in dry environments; the other is <a href="/wiki/Amniote" title="Amniote">amniotes</a>, whose living members are reptiles, birds and mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518-18">[17]</a></sup> One arthropod sub-group, <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, is the most species-rich member of all <a href="/wiki/Guild_(ecology)" title="Guild (ecology)">ecological guilds</a> in land and freshwater environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson1994CoevolutionaryProcess_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson1994CoevolutionaryProcess-17">[16]</a></sup> The lightest insects weigh less than 25 micrograms (millionths of a gram),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt-Nielsen1984InsectSize_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt-Nielsen1984InsectSize-19">[18]</a></sup> while the heaviest weigh over <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>70 grams (<span class="frac" role="math">2<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">2</span></span> oz).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> Some living <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malacostracan" title="Malacostracan">malacostracans</a> are much larger; for example, the legs of the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_spider_crab" title="Japanese spider crab">Japanese spider crab</a> may span up to 4 metres (13 ft),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt-Nielsen1984InsectSize_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt-Nielsen1984InsectSize-19">[18]</a></sup> with the heaviest of all living arthropods being the <a href="/wiki/American_lobster" title="American lobster">American lobster</a>, topping out at over 20 kg (44 lbs).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Segmentation">Segmentation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Segmentation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="noresize thumb tleft" style=";">
<div class="thumbinner" style="overflow:hidden;width:152px;">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:182px; overflow:hidden; position:relative; background-color:white;">

<div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HemimerusHanseni.jpg" title="File:HemimerusHanseni.jpg"> </a></div>Segments and <a href="/wiki/Tagma_(biology)" title="Tagma (biology)">tagmata</a> of an arthropod<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518-18">[17]</a></sup></div>
</div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20211214_arthropod_biramous_arthropodized_appendage_morphology_en.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/20211214_arthropod_biramous_arthropodized_appendage_morphology_en.png/300px-20211214_arthropod_biramous_arthropodized_appendage_morphology_en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/20211214_arthropod_biramous_arthropodized_appendage_morphology_en.png/450px-20211214_arthropod_biramous_arthropodized_appendage_morphology_en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/20211214_arthropod_biramous_arthropodized_appendage_morphology_en.png/600px-20211214_arthropod_biramous_arthropodized_appendage_morphology_en.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:20211214_arthropod_biramous_arthropodized_appendage_morphology_en.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biramous" title="Biramous">biramous</a> appendage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-21">[20]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryos</a> of all arthropods are segmented, built from a series of repeated modules. The <a href="/wiki/Most_recent_common_ancestor" title="Most recent common ancestor">last common ancestor</a> of living arthropods probably consisted of a series of undifferentiated segments, each with a pair of appendages that functioned as limbs. However, all known living and fossil arthropods have grouped segments into <a href="/wiki/Tagma_(biology)" title="Tagma (biology)">tagmata</a> in which segments and their limbs are specialized in various ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>The three-part appearance of many <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insect</a> bodies and the two-part appearance of <a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">spiders</a> is a result of this grouping;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WonderfulLifeP102To106_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-WonderfulLifeP102To106-22">[21]</a></sup> in fact there are no external signs of segmentation in <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518-18">[17]</a></sup> Arthropods also have two body elements that are not part of this serially repeated pattern of segments, an ocular somite at the front, where the mouth and eyes originated,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-linkinghub.elsevier.com_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-linkinghub.elsevier.com-23">[22]</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Telson" title="Telson">telson</a> at the rear, behind the <a href="/wiki/Anus" title="Anus">anus</a>.
</p><p>Originally it seems that each appendage-bearing segment had two separate pairs of appendages: an upper, unsegmented exite and a lower, segmented endopod. These would later fuse into a single pair of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biramous" title="Biramous">biramous</a> appendages united by a basal segment (protopod or basipod), with the upper branch acting as a <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gill</a> while the lower branch was used for locomotion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-21">[20]</a></sup> The appendages of most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crustaceans" title="Crustaceans">crustaceans</a> and some extinct taxa such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trilobites" title="Trilobites">trilobites</a> have another segmented branch known as exopods, but whether these structures have a single origin remain controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-21">[20]</a></sup> In some segments of all known arthropods the appendages have been modified, for example to form gills, mouth-parts, <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(biology)" title="Antenna (biology)">antennae</a> for collecting information,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WonderfulLifeP102To106_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-WonderfulLifeP102To106-22">[21]</a></sup> or claws for grasping;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> arthropods are "like <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Army_knife" title="Swiss Army knife">Swiss Army knives</a>, each equipped with a unique set of specialized tools."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518-18">[17]</a></sup> In many arthropods, appendages have vanished from some regions of the body; it is particularly common for abdominal appendages to have disappeared or be highly modified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:702px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20190913_Arthropoda_head_segments_appendages_en.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="530" data-file-width="1750" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/20190913_Arthropoda_head_segments_appendages_en.png/700px-20190913_Arthropoda_head_segments_appendages_en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/20190913_Arthropoda_head_segments_appendages_en.png/1050px-20190913_Arthropoda_head_segments_appendages_en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/20190913_Arthropoda_head_segments_appendages_en.png/1400px-20190913_Arthropoda_head_segments_appendages_en.png 2x" width="700"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:20190913_Arthropoda_head_segments_appendages_en.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alignment of anterior body segments and appendages across various arthropod taxa, based on the observations until mid 2010s. Head regions in black.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-linkinghub.elsevier.com_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-linkinghub.elsevier.com-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>The most conspicuous specialization of segments is in the head. The four major groups of arthropods – <a href="/wiki/Chelicerata" title="Chelicerata">Chelicerata</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sea_spider" title="Sea spider">sea spiders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe_crab" title="Horseshoe crab">horseshoe crabs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arachnid" title="Arachnid">arachnids</a>), <a href="/wiki/Myriapoda" title="Myriapoda">Myriapoda</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symphylan" title="Symphylan">symphylan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pauropod" title="Pauropod">pauropods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Millipede" title="Millipede">millipedes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Centipede" title="Centipede">centipedes</a>), <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">Crustacea</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oligostracan" title="Oligostracan">oligostracans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copepod" title="Copepod">copepods</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malacostracan" title="Malacostracan">malacostracans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Branchiopod" title="Branchiopod">branchiopods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hexapoda" title="Hexapoda">hexapods</a>, etc.), and the extinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trilobita" title="Trilobita">Trilobita</a> – have heads formed of various combinations of segments, with appendages that are missing or specialized in different ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518-18">[17]</a></sup> Despite myriapods and hexapods both having similar head combinations, hexapods are deeply nested within crustacea while myriapods are not, so these traits are believed to have evolved separately. In addition, some extinct arthropods, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Marrella" title="Marrella">Marrella</a></i>, belong to none of these groups, as their heads are formed by their own particular combinations of segments and specialized appendages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whittington1971Marrella_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whittington1971Marrella-30">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Working out the evolutionary stages by which all these different combinations could have appeared is so difficult that it has long been known as "the <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_head_problem" title="Arthropod head problem">arthropod head problem</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> In 1960, R. E. Snodgrass even hoped it would not be solved, as he found trying to work out solutions to be fun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[Note 2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exoskeleton">Exoskeleton</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Exoskeleton">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_exoskeleton" title="Arthropod exoskeleton">Arthropod exoskeleton</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arthropod_cuticle_based_upon_Xvazquez_edited_to_include_english_legend.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1224" data-file-width="484" decoding="async" height="556" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Arthropod_cuticle_based_upon_Xvazquez_edited_to_include_english_legend.PNG/220px-Arthropod_cuticle_based_upon_Xvazquez_edited_to_include_english_legend.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Arthropod_cuticle_based_upon_Xvazquez_edited_to_include_english_legend.PNG/330px-Arthropod_cuticle_based_upon_Xvazquez_edited_to_include_english_legend.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Arthropod_cuticle_based_upon_Xvazquez_edited_to_include_english_legend.PNG/440px-Arthropod_cuticle_based_upon_Xvazquez_edited_to_include_english_legend.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arthropod_cuticle_based_upon_Xvazquez_edited_to_include_english_legend.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of an idealized arthropod exoskeleton.</div></div></div>
<p>Arthropod exoskeletons are made of <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_cuticle" title="Arthropod cuticle">cuticle</a>, a non-cellular material secreted by the <a href="/wiki/Epidermis_(zoology)" title="Epidermis (zoology)">epidermis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518_18-8"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518-18">[17]</a></sup> Their cuticles vary in the details of their structure, but generally consist of three main layers: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epicuticle" title="Epicuticle">epicuticle</a>, a thin outer <a href="/wiki/Wax" title="Wax">waxy</a> coat that moisture-proofs the other layers and gives them some protection; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exocuticle" title="Exocuticle">exocuticle</a>, which consists of <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a> and chemically hardened <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>; and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endocuticle" title="Endocuticle">endocuticle</a>, which consists of chitin and unhardened proteins. The exocuticle and endocuticle together are known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Procuticle" title="Procuticle">procuticle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mechanical_Design_in_Organisms_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mechanical_Design_in_Organisms-34">[32]</a></sup> Each body segment and limb section is encased in hardened cuticle. The joints between body segments and between limb sections are covered by flexible cuticle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518_18-9"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>The exoskeletons of most aquatic <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a> are <a href="/wiki/Biomineralization" title="Biomineralization">biomineralized</a> with <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a> extracted from the water. Some terrestrial crustaceans have developed means of storing the mineral, since on land they cannot rely on a steady supply of dissolved calcium carbonate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup> Biomineralization generally affects the exocuticle and the outer part of the endocuticle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mechanical_Design_in_Organisms_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mechanical_Design_in_Organisms-34">[32]</a></sup> Two recent hypotheses about the evolution of biomineralization in arthropods and other groups of animals propose that it provides tougher defensive armor,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dzik2007_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dzik2007-36">[34]</a></sup> and that it allows animals to grow larger and stronger by providing more rigid skeletons;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen2005_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2005-37">[35]</a></sup> and in either case a mineral-organic <a href="/wiki/Composite_material" title="Composite material">composite</a> exoskeleton is cheaper to build than an all-organic one of comparable strength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen2005_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2005-37">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bengtson2004_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bengtson2004-38">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>The cuticle may have setae (bristles) growing from special cells in the epidermis. Setae are as varied in form and function as appendages. For example, they are often used as sensors to detect air or water currents, or contact with objects; aquatic arthropods use <a href="/wiki/Feather" title="Feather">feather</a>-like setae to increase the surface area of swimming appendages and to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filter_feeding" title="Filter feeding">filter</a> food particles out of water; aquatic insects, which are air-breathers, use thick <a href="/wiki/Felt" title="Felt">felt</a>-like coats of setae to trap air, extending the time they can spend under water; heavy, rigid setae serve as defensive spines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518_18-10"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although all arthropods use muscles attached to the inside of the exoskeleton to flex their limbs, some still use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydraulic" title="Hydraulic">hydraulic</a> pressure to extend them, a system inherited from their pre-arthropod ancestors;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> for example, all spiders extend their legs hydraulically and can generate pressures up to eight times their resting level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moulting">Moulting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Moulting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ecdysis" title="Ecdysis">Ecdysis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cicada_climbing_out_of_its_exoskeleton_while_attached_to_tree.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="956" data-file-width="884" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Cicada_climbing_out_of_its_exoskeleton_while_attached_to_tree.jpg/220px-Cicada_climbing_out_of_its_exoskeleton_while_attached_to_tree.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Cicada_climbing_out_of_its_exoskeleton_while_attached_to_tree.jpg/330px-Cicada_climbing_out_of_its_exoskeleton_while_attached_to_tree.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Cicada_climbing_out_of_its_exoskeleton_while_attached_to_tree.jpg/440px-Cicada_climbing_out_of_its_exoskeleton_while_attached_to_tree.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cicada_climbing_out_of_its_exoskeleton_while_attached_to_tree.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cicada" title="Cicada">Cicada</a> climbing out of its exoskeleton while attached to tree</div></div></div>
<p>The exoskeleton cannot stretch and thus restricts growth. Arthropods, therefore, replace their exoskeletons by undergoing <a href="/wiki/Ecdysis" title="Ecdysis">ecdysis</a> (moulting), or shedding the old exoskeleton after growing a new one that is not yet hardened. Moulting cycles run nearly continuously until an arthropod reaches full size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P523To524_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P523To524-41">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The developmental stages between each moult (ecdysis) until sexual maturity is reached is called an <a href="/wiki/Instar" title="Instar">instar</a>. Differences between instars can often be seen in altered body proportions, colors, patterns, changes in the number of body segments or head width. After moulting, i.e. shedding their exoskeleton, the juvenile arthropods continue in their life cycle until they either pupate or moult again.
</p><p>In the initial phase of moulting, the animal stops feeding and its epidermis releases moulting fluid, a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> that digests the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endocuticle" title="Endocuticle">endocuticle</a> and thus detaches the old cuticle. This phase begins when the <a href="/wiki/Epidermis_(zoology)" title="Epidermis (zoology)">epidermis</a> has secreted a new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epicuticle" title="Epicuticle">epicuticle</a> to protect it from the enzymes, and the epidermis secretes the new exocuticle while the old cuticle is detaching. When this stage is complete, the animal makes its body swell by taking in a large quantity of water or air, and this makes the old cuticle split along predefined weaknesses where the old exocuticle was thinnest. It commonly takes several minutes for the animal to struggle out of the old cuticle. At this point, the new one is wrinkled and so soft that the animal cannot support itself and finds it very difficult to move, and the new endocuticle has not yet formed. The animal continues to pump itself up to stretch the new cuticle as much as possible, then hardens the new exocuticle and eliminates the excess air or water. By the end of this phase, the new endocuticle has formed. Many arthropods then eat the discarded cuticle to reclaim its materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P523To524_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P523To524-41">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because arthropods are unprotected and nearly immobilized until the new cuticle has hardened, they are in danger both of being trapped in the old cuticle and of being attacked by predators. Moulting may be responsible for 80 to 90% of all arthropod deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P523To524_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P523To524-41">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_organs">Internal organs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Internal organs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="noresize thumb tright" style=";">
<div class="thumbinner" style="overflow:hidden;width:202px;">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:147px; overflow:hidden; position:relative; background-color:white;">
