<div style="text-align:left; background-color:transparent; line-height:110%;">
<div id="annotation_5x65" style="position:absolute; left:5px; top:65px; line-height:110%;"><span style="background-color:transparent;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="border: none;background-color:red; color:black;"> </span> = heart</span></span></div>
<div id="annotation_5x85" style="position:absolute; left:5px; top:85px; line-height:110%;"><span style="background-color:transparent;"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="border: none;background-color:yellow; color:black;"> </span> = gut</span></span></div>
<div id="annotation_5x105" style="position:absolute; left:5px; top:105px; line-height:110%;"><span style="background-color:transparent;"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="border: none;background-color:blue; color:white;"> </span> = brain / <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganglia" title="Ganglia">ganglia</a></span></span></div>
<div id="annotation_5x125" style="position:absolute; left:5px; top:125px; line-height:110%;"><span style="background-color:transparent;"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="border: none;"><span class="legend-text" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;"><span style="color:blue"> O</span></span></span> = eye</span></span></div>
</div>
<div style="visibility:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arthropod_body_struct_01.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="61" data-file-width="199" decoding="async" height="61" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/66/Arthropod_body_struct_01.png" width="199"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arthropod_body_struct_01.png" title="File:Arthropod body struct 01.png"> </a></div>Basic arthropod body structure</div>
</div></div>
<p>Arthropod bodies are also segmented internally, and the nervous, muscular, circulatory, and excretory systems have repeated components.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518_18-11"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518-18">[17]</a></sup> Arthropods come from a lineage of animals that have a <a href="/wiki/Coelom" title="Coelom">coelom</a>, a membrane-lined cavity between the gut and the body wall that accommodates the internal organs. The strong, segmented limbs of arthropods eliminate the need for one of the coelom's main ancestral functions, as a <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_skeleton" title="Hydrostatic skeleton">hydrostatic skeleton</a>, which muscles compress in order to change the animal's shape and thus enable it to move. Hence the coelom of the arthropod is reduced to small areas around the reproductive and excretory systems. Its place is largely taken by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemocoel" title="Hemocoel">hemocoel</a>, a cavity that runs most of the length of the body and through which <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a> flows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P527To528_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P527To528-42">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Respiration_and_circulation">Respiration and circulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Respiration and circulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hemolymph" title="Hemolymph">Hemolymph</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemocyte" title="Hemocyte">hemocyte</a></div>
<p>Arthropods have open <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory systems</a>, although most have a few short, open-ended <a href="/wiki/Artery" title="Artery">arteries</a>. In chelicerates and crustaceans, the blood carries <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> to the tissues, while <a href="/wiki/Hexapoda" title="Hexapoda">hexapods</a> use a separate system of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invertebrate_trachea" title="Invertebrate trachea">tracheae</a>. Many crustaceans, but few chelicerates and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tracheata" title="Tracheata">tracheates</a>, use <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_pigment" title="Respiratory pigment">respiratory pigments</a> to assist oxygen transport. The most common respiratory pigment in arthropods is <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Hemocyanin" title="Hemocyanin">hemocyanin</a>; this is used by many crustaceans and a few <a href="/wiki/Centipede" title="Centipede">centipedes</a>. A few crustaceans and insects use iron-based <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>, the respiratory pigment used by <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>. As with other invertebrates, the respiratory pigments of those arthropods that have them are generally dissolved in the blood and rarely enclosed in <a href="/wiki/Blood_cell" title="Blood cell">corpuscles</a> as they are in vertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P527To528_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P527To528-42">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>The heart is typically a muscular tube that runs just under the back and for most of the length of the hemocoel. It contracts in ripples that run from rear to front, pushing blood forwards. Sections not being squeezed by the heart muscle are expanded either by elastic <a href="/wiki/Ligament" title="Ligament">ligaments</a> or by small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscles</a>, in either case connecting the heart to the body wall. Along the heart run a series of paired ostia, non-return valves that allow blood to enter the heart but prevent it from leaving before it reaches the front.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P527To528_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P527To528-42">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Arthropods have a wide variety of respiratory systems. Small species often do not have any, since their high ratio of surface area to volume enables simple diffusion through the body surface to supply enough oxygen. Crustacea usually have gills that are modified appendages. Many arachnids have <a href="/wiki/Book_lung" title="Book lung">book lungs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garwood_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garwood-43">[41]</a></sup> Tracheae, systems of branching tunnels that run from the openings in the body walls, deliver oxygen directly to individual cells in many insects, myriapods and <a href="/wiki/Arachnid" title="Arachnid">arachnids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nervous_system">Nervous system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Nervous system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Living arthropods have paired main nerve cords running along their bodies below the gut, and in each segment the cords form a pair of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganglia" title="Ganglia">ganglia</a> from which <a href="/wiki/Sensory_nerve" title="Sensory nerve">sensory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Motor_nerve" title="Motor nerve">motor</a> nerves run to other parts of the segment. Although the pairs of ganglia in each segment often appear physically fused, they are connected by <a href="/wiki/Commissure" title="Commissure">commissures</a> (relatively large bundles of nerves), which give arthropod nervous systems a characteristic "ladder-like" appearance. The brain is in the head, encircling and mainly <i>above</i> the esophagus. It consists of the fused ganglia of the acron and one or two of the foremost segments that form the head – a total of three pairs of ganglia in most arthropods, but only two in chelicerates, which do not have antennae or the ganglion connected to them. The ganglia of other head segments are often close to the brain and function as part of it. In insects these other head ganglia combine into a pair of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subesophageal_ganglia" title="Subesophageal ganglia">subesophageal ganglia</a>, under and behind the esophagus. Spiders take this process a step further, as <i>all</i> the <a href="/wiki/Segmental_ganglia" title="Segmental ganglia">segmental ganglia</a> are incorporated into the subesophageal ganglia, which occupy most of the space in the cephalothorax (front "super-segment").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P531To532_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P531To532-45">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Excretory_system">Excretory system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Excretory system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are two different types of arthropod excretory systems. In aquatic arthropods, the end-product of biochemical reactions that <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolise</a> <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> is <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, which is so toxic that it needs to be diluted as much as possible with water. The ammonia is then eliminated via any permeable membrane, mainly through the gills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garwood_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Garwood-43">[41]</a></sup> All crustaceans use this system, and its high consumption of water may be responsible for the relative lack of success of crustaceans as land animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P529To530_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P529To530-46">[44]</a></sup> Various groups of terrestrial arthropods have independently developed a different system: the end-product of nitrogen metabolism is <a href="/wiki/Uric_acid" title="Uric acid">uric acid</a>, which can be excreted as dry material; the <a href="/wiki/Malpighian_tubule_system" title="Malpighian tubule system">Malpighian tubule system</a> filters the uric acid and other nitrogenous waste out of the blood in the hemocoel, and dumps these materials into the hindgut, from which they are expelled as <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">feces</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P529To530_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P529To530-46">[44]</a></sup> Most aquatic arthropods and some terrestrial ones also have organs called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nephridia" title="Nephridia">nephridia</a> ("little <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidneys</a>"), which extract other wastes for excretion as <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P529To530_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P529To530-46">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Senses">Senses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Senses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The stiff <a href="/wiki/Cuticle" title="Cuticle">cuticles</a> of arthropods would block out information about the outside world, except that they are penetrated by many sensors or connections from sensors to the nervous system. In fact, arthropods have modified their cuticles into elaborate arrays of sensors. Various touch sensors, mostly <a href="/wiki/Seta" title="Seta">setae</a>, respond to different levels of force, from strong contact to very weak air currents. Chemical sensors provide equivalents of <a href="/wiki/Taste" title="Taste">taste</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olfaction" title="Olfaction">smell</a>, often by means of setae. Pressure sensors often take the form of membranes that function as <a href="/wiki/Eardrum" title="Eardrum">eardrums</a>, but are connected directly to nerves rather than to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auditory_ossicle" title="Auditory ossicle">auditory ossicles</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(biology)" title="Antenna (biology)">antennae</a> of most hexapods include sensor packages that monitor <a href="/wiki/Humidity" title="Humidity">humidity</a>, moisture and temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537-47">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most arthropods lack balance and <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">acceleration</a> sensors, and rely on their eyes to tell them which way is up. The self-righting behavior of <a href="/wiki/Cockroach" title="Cockroach">cockroaches</a> is triggered when pressure sensors on the underside of the feet report no pressure. However, many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malacostracan" title="Malacostracan">malacostracan</a> crustaceans have <a href="/wiki/Statocyst" title="Statocyst">statocysts</a>, which provide the same sort of information as the balance and motion sensors of the vertebrate <a href="/wiki/Inner_ear" title="Inner ear">inner ear</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537-47">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proprioceptor" title="Proprioceptor">proprioceptors</a> of arthropods, sensors that report the force exerted by muscles and the degree of bending in the body and joints, are well understood. However, little is known about what other internal sensors arthropods may have.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537-47">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Optical">Optical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Optical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_eye" title="Arthropod eye">Arthropod eye</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arthropoda_montage_(eyes).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2147" data-file-width="3220" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Arthropoda_montage_%28eyes%29.png/300px-Arthropoda_montage_%28eyes%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Arthropoda_montage_%28eyes%29.png/450px-Arthropoda_montage_%28eyes%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Arthropoda_montage_%28eyes%29.png/600px-Arthropoda_montage_%28eyes%29.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arthropoda_montage_(eyes).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Arthropod eyes</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wasp_ocelli.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="207" data-file-width="426" decoding="async" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4e/Wasp_ocelli.JPG/240px-Wasp_ocelli.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4e/Wasp_ocelli.JPG/360px-Wasp_ocelli.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/4e/Wasp_ocelli.JPG 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wasp_ocelli.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Head of a <a href="/wiki/Wasp" title="Wasp">wasp</a> with three ocelli (center), and compound eyes at the left and right</div></div></div>
<p>Most arthropods have sophisticated visual systems that include one or more usually both of <a href="/wiki/Compound_eye" title="Compound eye">compound eyes</a> and pigment-cup <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocelli" title="Ocelli">ocelli</a> ("little eyes"). In most cases ocelli are only capable of detecting the direction from which light is coming, using the shadow cast by the walls of the cup. However, the main eyes of <a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">spiders</a> are pigment-cup ocelli that are capable of forming images,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537-47">[45]</a></sup> and those of <a href="/wiki/Jumping_spider" title="Jumping spider">jumping spiders</a> can rotate to track prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Compound eyes consist of fifteen to several thousand independent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ommatidia" title="Ommatidia">ommatidia</a>, columns that are usually <a href="/wiki/Hexagon" title="Hexagon">hexagonal</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cross_section_(geometry)" title="Cross section (geometry)">cross section</a>. Each ommatidium is an independent sensor, with its own light-sensitive cells and often with its own <a href="/wiki/Lens_(anatomy)" title="Lens (anatomy)">lens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornea" title="Cornea">cornea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537_47-4"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537-47">[45]</a></sup> Compound eyes have a wide field of view, and can detect fast movement and, in some cases, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_polarization" title="Light polarization">polarization of light</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup> On the other hand, the relatively large size of ommatidia makes the images rather coarse, and compound eyes are shorter-sighted than those of birds and mammals – although this is not a severe disadvantage, as objects and events within 20 cm (8 in) are most important to most arthropods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537_47-5"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537-47">[45]</a></sup> Several arthropods have color vision, and that of some insects has been studied in detail; for example, the ommatidia of bees contain receptors for both green and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultra-violet" title="Ultra-violet">ultra-violet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537_47-6"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537-47">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Olfaction">Olfaction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Olfaction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Insect_olfaction" title="Insect olfaction">Insect olfaction</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction_and_development">Reproduction and development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Reproduction and development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scorpionwithyoung.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="722" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Scorpionwithyoung.JPG/200px-Scorpionwithyoung.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Scorpionwithyoung.JPG/300px-Scorpionwithyoung.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Scorpionwithyoung.JPG/400px-Scorpionwithyoung.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scorpionwithyoung.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Compsobuthus_werneri" title="Compsobuthus werneri">Compsobuthus werneri</a></i> female with young (white)</div></div></div>
<p>A few arthropods, such as <a href="/wiki/Barnacle" title="Barnacle">barnacles</a>, are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermaphroditic" title="Hermaphroditic">hermaphroditic</a>, that is, each can have the organs of both <a href="/wiki/Sex" title="Sex">sexes</a>. However, individuals of most species remain of one sex their entire lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P537To539_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P537To539-50">[48]</a></sup> A few species of <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> and crustaceans can reproduce by <a href="/wiki/Parthenogenesis" title="Parthenogenesis">parthenogenesis</a>, especially if conditions favor a "population explosion". However, most arthropods rely on <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">sexual reproduction</a>, and parthenogenetic species often revert to sexual reproduction when conditions become less favorable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olive2001ReproductionInvertebratesInEncOfLifeSci_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olive2001ReproductionInvertebratesInEncOfLifeSci-51">[49]</a></sup>  The ability to undergo <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a> is widespread among arthropods including both those that reproduce sexually and those that reproduce <a href="/wiki/Parthenogenesis" title="Parthenogenesis">parthenogenetically</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup> Although meiosis is a major characteristic of arthropods, understanding of its fundamental adaptive benefit has long been regarded as an unresolved problem,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup> that appears to have remained unsettled.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aquatic_animal" title="Aquatic animal">Aquatic</a> arthropods may breed by external fertilization, as for example <a href="/wiki/Frog" title="Frog">frogs</a> do, or by <a href="/wiki/Internal_fertilization" title="Internal fertilization">internal fertilization</a>, where the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovum" title="Ovum">ova</a> remain in the female's body and the <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a> must somehow be inserted. All known terrestrial arthropods use internal fertilization. <a href="/wiki/Opiliones" title="Opiliones">Opiliones</a> (harvestmen), <a href="/wiki/Millipede" title="Millipede">millipedes</a>, and some crustaceans use modified appendages such as <a href="/wiki/Gonopod" title="Gonopod">gonopods</a> or <a href="/wiki/Opiliones_penis" title="Opiliones penis">penises</a> to transfer the sperm directly to the female. However, most male <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_animal" title="Terrestrial animal">terrestrial</a> arthropods produce <a href="/wiki/Spermatophore" title="Spermatophore">spermatophores</a>, waterproof packets of <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a>, which the females take into their bodies. A few such species rely on females to find spermatophores that have already been deposited on the ground, but in most cases males only deposit spermatophores when complex <a href="/wiki/Courtship" title="Courtship">courtship</a> rituals look likely to be successful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P537To539_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P537To539-50">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shrimp_nauplius.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="588" data-file-width="590" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Shrimp_nauplius.jpg/200px-Shrimp_nauplius.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Shrimp_nauplius.jpg/300px-Shrimp_nauplius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Shrimp_nauplius.jpg/400px-Shrimp_nauplius.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shrimp_nauplius.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The nauplius larva of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penaeid_shrimp" title="Penaeid shrimp">penaeid shrimp</a></div></div></div>
<p>Most arthropods lay eggs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P537To539_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P537To539-50">[48]</a></sup> but scorpions are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovoviparity" title="Ovoviparity">ovoviparous</a>: they produce live young after the eggs have hatched inside the mother, and are noted for prolonged maternal care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup> Newly born arthropods have diverse forms, and insects alone cover the range of extremes. Some hatch as apparently miniature adults (direct development), and in some cases, such as <a href="/wiki/Silverfish" title="Silverfish">silverfish</a>, the hatchlings do not feed and may be helpless until after their first moult. Many insects hatch as grubs or <a href="/wiki/Caterpillar" title="Caterpillar">caterpillars</a>, which do not have segmented limbs or hardened cuticles, and <a href="/wiki/Metamorphosis" title="Metamorphosis">metamorphose</a> into adult forms by entering an inactive phase in which the larval tissues are broken down and re-used to build the adult body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dragonfly" title="Dragonfly">Dragonfly</a> larvae have the typical cuticles and jointed limbs of arthropods but are flightless water-breathers with extendable jaws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup> Crustaceans commonly hatch as tiny <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nauplius_(larva)" title="Nauplius (larva)">nauplius</a> larvae that have only three segments and pairs of appendages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P537To539_50-3"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P537To539-50">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_history">Evolutionary history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Evolutionary history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogeny_of_insects" title="Phylogeny of insects">Phylogeny of insects</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_common_ancestor">Last common ancestor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Last common ancestor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Based on the distribution of shared <a href="/wiki/Plesiomorphy_and_symplesiomorphy" title="Plesiomorphy and symplesiomorphy">plesiomorphic</a> features in extant and fossil taxa, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_common_ancestor" title="Last common ancestor">last common ancestor</a> of all arthropods is inferred to have been as a modular organism with each module covered by its own <a href="/wiki/Sclerite" title="Sclerite">sclerite</a> (armor plate) and bearing a pair of biramous <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_leg" title="Arthropod leg">limbs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-car-epnla_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-car-epnla-57">[55]</a></sup> However, whether the ancestral limb was <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_leg#Biramous_and_uniramous" title="Arthropod leg">uniramous or biramous</a> is far from a settled debate.
This Ur-arthropod had a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ventral#Dorsal_and_ventral" title="Ventral">ventral</a> mouth, pre-oral antennae and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ventral#Dorsal_and_ventral" title="Ventral">dorsal</a> eyes at the front of the body. It was assumed to have been a non-discriminatory <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediment</a> feeder, processing whatever sediment came its way for food,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-car-epnla_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-car-epnla-57">[55]</a></sup> but fossil findings hint that the last common ancestor of both arthropods and <a href="/wiki/Priapulida" title="Priapulida">priapulida</a> shared the same specialized mouth apparatus; a circular mouth with rings of teeth used for capturing animal prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_record">Fossil record</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Fossil record">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marrella.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="713" data-file-width="721" decoding="async" height="119" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Marrella.jpg/120px-Marrella.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Marrella.jpg/180px-Marrella.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Marrella.jpg/240px-Marrella.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marrella.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Marrella" title="Marrella">Marrella</a></i>, one of the puzzling arthropods from the <a href="/wiki/Burgess_Shale" title="Burgess Shale">Burgess Shale</a></div></div></div>
<p>It has been proposed that the <a href="/wiki/Ediacaran" title="Ediacaran">Ediacaran</a> animals <i><a href="/wiki/Parvancorina" title="Parvancorina">Parvancorina</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Spriggina" title="Spriggina">Spriggina</a></i>, from around <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=555">555</a> million years ago</span>, were arthropods,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup> but later study shows that their affinities of being origin of arthropods are not reliable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup> Small arthropods with bivalve-like shells have been found in Early Cambrian fossil beds dating <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=541–539">541 to 539</a> million years ago</span> in China and Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Braun2007_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Braun2007-63">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup> The earliest Cambrian <a href="/wiki/Trilobite" title="Trilobite">trilobite</a> fossils are about 530 million years old, but the class was already quite diverse and worldwide, suggesting that they had been around for quite some time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lieberman1999Trilobites_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lieberman1999Trilobites-67">[65]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Maotianshan_Shales" title="Maotianshan Shales">Maotianshan shales</a>, which date to between 530 and 520 million years ago, fossils of arthropods such as <i><a href="/wiki/Kylinxia" title="Kylinxia">Kylinxia</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Erratus" title="Erratus">Erratus</a></i> have been found that seem to show a transitional split between lobopodia and other more primitive stem arthropods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phys-Kylinxia_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-phys-Kylinxia-68">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fu2022_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-fu2022-69">[67]</a></sup> Re-examination in the 1970s of the <a href="/wiki/Burgess_Shale" title="Burgess Shale">Burgess Shale</a> fossils from about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=505">505</a> million years ago</span> identified many arthropods, some of which could not be assigned to any of the well-known groups, and thus intensified the debate about the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">Cambrian explosion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whittington1979Lawn_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whittington1979Lawn-70">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whittington1985BurgessShale_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whittington1985BurgessShale-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WonderfulLife_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-WonderfulLife-72">[70]</a></sup> A fossil of <i><a href="/wiki/Marrella" title="Marrella">Marrella</a></i> from the Burgess Shale has provided the earliest clear evidence of <a href="/wiki/Moulting" title="Moulting">moulting</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20210310_Kylinxia_zhangi.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="2150" decoding="async" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/20210310_Kylinxia_zhangi.png/200px-20210310_Kylinxia_zhangi.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/20210310_Kylinxia_zhangi.png/300px-20210310_Kylinxia_zhangi.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/20210310_Kylinxia_zhangi.png/400px-20210310_Kylinxia_zhangi.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:20210310_Kylinxia_zhangi.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Kylinxia" title="Kylinxia">Kylinxia</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phys-Kylinxia_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-phys-Kylinxia-68">[66]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The earliest fossil crustaceans date from about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=511">511</a> million years ago</span> in the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">Cambrian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup> and fossil <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimp</a> from about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=500">500</a> million years ago</span> apparently formed a tight-knit procession across the seabed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup> Crustacean fossils are common from the <a href="/wiki/Ordovician" title="Ordovician">Ordovician</a> period onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup> They have remained almost entirely aquatic, possibly because they never developed <a href="/wiki/Excretory_system" title="Excretory system">excretory systems</a> that conserve water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P529To530_46-3"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P529To530-46">[44]</a></sup> In 2020 scientists announced the discovery of <i><a href="/wiki/Kylinxia" title="Kylinxia">Kylinxia</a></i>, a five-eyed ~5 cm long shrimp-like animal living <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">518 Mya</a> that – with multiple distinctive features – appears to be a key ‘<a href="/wiki/Transitional_fossil" title="Transitional fossil">missing link</a>’ of the evolution from <i><a href="/wiki/Anomalocaris" title="Anomalocaris">Anomalocaris</a></i> to true arthropods and could be at the evolutionary root of true arthropods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phys-Kylinxia_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-phys-Kylinxia-68">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Arthropods provide the earliest identifiable fossils of land animals, from about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=419">419</a> million years ago</span> in the Late <a href="/wiki/Silurian" title="Silurian">Silurian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garwood_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-Garwood-43">[41]</a></sup> and terrestrial tracks from about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=450">450</a> million years ago</span> appear to have been made by arthropods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup> Arthropods possessed attributes that were easy <a href="/wiki/Exaptation" title="Exaptation">coopted</a> for life on land; their existing jointed exoskeletons provided protection against desiccation, support against gravity and a means of locomotion that was not dependent on water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CowenHistLifeEd3P126_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-CowenHistLifeEd3P126-79">[77]</a></sup> Around the same time the aquatic, scorpion-like <a href="/wiki/Eurypterid" title="Eurypterid">eurypterids</a> became the largest ever arthropods, some as long as 2.5 m (8 ft 2 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest known <a href="/wiki/Arachnid" title="Arachnid">arachnid</a> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trigonotarbid" title="Trigonotarbid">trigonotarbid</a> <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Palaeotarbus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Palaeotarbus (page does not exist)">Palaeotarbus</a> jerami</i>, from about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=420">420</a> million years ago</span> in the <a href="/wiki/Silurian" title="Silurian">Silurian</a> period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[Note 3]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Attercopus" title="Attercopus">Attercopus</a> fimbriunguis</i>, from <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=386">386</a> million years ago</span> in the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> period, bears the earliest known silk-producing spigots, but its lack of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinneret_(spider)" title="Spinneret (spider)">spinnerets</a> means it was not one of the true <a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">spiders</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup> which first appear in the Late <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> over <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=299">299</a> million years ago</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> periods provide a large number of fossil spiders, including representatives of many modern families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VollrathSelden2007BehaviorInEvolutionOfSpiders_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-VollrathSelden2007BehaviorInEvolutionOfSpiders-86">[83]</a></sup> Fossils of aquatic <a href="/wiki/Scorpion" title="Scorpion">scorpions</a> with <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gills</a> appear in the Silurian and <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> periods, and the earliest fossil of an air-breathing scorpion with <a href="/wiki/Book_lung" title="Book lung">book lungs</a> dates from the Early Carboniferous period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest possible insect fossil is the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhyniognatha_hirsti" title="Rhyniognatha hirsti">Rhyniognatha hirsti</a></i>, dated at <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=396–407">396 to 407</a> million years ago</span>, but its <a href="/wiki/Mandible_(insect_mouthpart)" title="Mandible (insect mouthpart)">mandibles</a> are of a type found only in winged insects, which suggests that the earliest insects appeared in the Silurian period,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EngelGrim2004_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-EngelGrim2004-88">[85]</a></sup> although later study shows possibility that <i>Rhyniognatha</i> can be myriapod, not an insect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazon_Creek_fossils" title="Mazon Creek fossils">Mazon Creek lagerstätten</a> from the Late Carboniferous, about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=300">300</a> million years ago</span>, include about 200 species, some gigantic by modern standards, and indicate that insects had occupied their main modern <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niches</a> as <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Detritivore" title="Detritivore">detritivores</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insectivore" title="Insectivore">insectivores</a>. Social <a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">termites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ants</a> first appear in the Early <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a>, and advanced social bees have been found in Late Cretaceous rocks but did not become abundant until the Middle <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_family_tree">Evolutionary family tree</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Evolutionary family tree">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Onycophora.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="912" data-file-width="924" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Onycophora.jpg/220px-Onycophora.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Onycophora.jpg/330px-Onycophora.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Onycophora.jpg/440px-Onycophora.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Onycophora.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Velvet_worm" title="Velvet worm">velvet worm (Onychophora)</a> is closely related to arthropods<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Budd1996_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Budd1996-91">[88]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>From 1952 to 1977, zoologist <a href="/wiki/Sidnie_Manton" title="Sidnie Manton">Sidnie Manton</a> and others argued that arthropods are <a href="/wiki/Polyphyly" title="Polyphyly">polyphyletic</a>, in other words, that they do not share a common ancestor that was itself an arthropod. Instead, they proposed that three separate groups of "arthropods" evolved separately from common worm-like ancestors: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chelicerate" title="Chelicerate">chelicerates</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">spiders</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scorpion" title="Scorpion">scorpions</a>; the crustaceans; and the <a href="/wiki/Uniramia" title="Uniramia">uniramia</a>, consisting of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Onychophoran" title="Onychophoran">onychophorans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myriapod" title="Myriapod">myriapods</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hexapoda" title="Hexapoda">hexapods</a>. These arguments usually bypassed <a href="/wiki/Trilobite" title="Trilobite">trilobites</a>, as the evolutionary relationships of this class were unclear. Proponents of polyphyly argued the following: that the similarities between these groups are the results of <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a>, as natural consequences of having rigid, segmented <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">exoskeletons</a>; that the three groups use different chemical means of hardening the cuticle; that there were significant differences in the construction of their compound eyes; that it is hard to see how such different configurations of segments and appendages in the head could have evolved from the same ancestor; and that crustaceans have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biramous" title="Biramous">biramous</a> limbs with separate gill and leg branches, while the other two groups have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uniramous" title="Uniramous">uniramous</a> limbs in which the single branch serves as a leg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="float:right; width:auto; max-width:100%; border:solid 1px silver; padding:2px; margin:2px; font-size:80%;">

</td>
<td class="clade-leaf" rowspan="2">
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anomalocarid" title="Anomalocarid">anomalocarid</a>-like taxa,<br/>including modern <a href="/wiki/Tardigrade" title="Tardigrade">tardigrades</a> as<br/>well as extinct animals like<br/><i><a href="/wiki/Kerygmachela" title="Kerygmachela">Kerygmachela</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Opabinia" title="Opabinia">Opabinia</a></i>
</p>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel"> 
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-label"> 
</td>
<td class="clade-leaf" rowspan="2">
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r984819063" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>
<table class="clade">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="clade-label first"> 
</td>
<td class="clade-leaf" rowspan="2">
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Anomalocaris" title="Anomalocaris">Anomalocaris</a></i>
</p>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel"> 
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-label"> 
</td>
<td class="clade-leaf" rowspan="2">
<p>arthropods,<br/>including living groups and<br/>extinct forms such as <a href="/wiki/Trilobite" title="Trilobite">trilobites</a>
</p>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel last"> 
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel last"> 
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel last"> 
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel last"> 
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel last"> 
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel last"> 
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</div>Simplified summary of Budd's "broad-scale" cladogram (1996)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Budd1996_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Budd1996-91">[88]</a></sup></div>
<p>Further analysis and discoveries in the 1990s reversed this view, and led to acceptance that arthropods are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophyletic" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a>, in other words they are inferred to share a common ancestor that was itself an arthropod.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Graham_Budd" title="Graham Budd">Graham Budd</a>'s analyses of <i><a href="/wiki/Kerygmachela" title="Kerygmachela">Kerygmachela</a></i> in 1993 and of <i><a href="/wiki/Opabinia" title="Opabinia">Opabinia</a></i> in 1996 convinced him that these animals were similar to onychophorans and to various Early <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">Cambrian</a> "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lobopod" title="Lobopod">lobopods</a>", and he presented an "evolutionary family tree" that showed these as "aunts" and "cousins" of all arthropods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Budd1996_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-Budd1996-91">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Budd1993_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Budd1993-95">[92]</a></sup> These changes made the scope of the term "arthropod" unclear, and Claus Nielsen proposed that the wider group should be labelled "<a href="/wiki/Panarthropoda" title="Panarthropoda">Panarthropoda</a>" ("all the arthropods") while the animals with jointed limbs and hardened cuticles should be called "Euarthropoda" ("true arthropods").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>A contrary view was presented in 2003, when Jan Bergström and Xian-Guang Hou argued that, if arthropods were a "sister-group" to any of the anomalocarids, they must have lost and then re-evolved features that were well-developed in the anomalocarids. The earliest known arthropods ate mud in order to extract food particles from it, and possessed variable numbers of segments with unspecialized appendages that functioned as both gills and legs. Anomalocarids were, by the standards of the time, huge and sophisticated predators with specialized mouths and grasping appendages, fixed numbers of segments some of which were specialized, tail fins, and gills that were very different from those of arthropods. In 2006, they suggested that arthropods were more closely related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lobopod" title="Lobopod">lobopods</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tardigrade" title="Tardigrade">tardigrades</a> than to anomalocarids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[94]</a></sup> In 2014, research indicated that tardigrades were more closely related to arthropods than velvet worms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup>
</p>

</div>Relationships of Ecdysozoa to each other and to annelids, etc.,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TelfordBourlatEtAl2008EvolutionOfEcdysozoa_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-TelfordBourlatEtAl2008EvolutionOfEcdysozoa-99">[96]</a></sup> including euthycarcinoids<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaccari2004_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vaccari2004-100">[97]</a></sup></div>
<p>Higher up the "family tree", the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annelida" title="Annelida">Annelida</a> have traditionally been considered the closest relatives of the Panarthropoda, since both groups have segmented bodies, and the combination of these groups was labelled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Articulata_Hypothesis" title="Articulata Hypothesis">Articulata</a>. There had been competing proposals that arthropods were closely related to other groups such as <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematodes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Priapulid" title="Priapulid">priapulids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tardigrade" title="Tardigrade">tardigrades</a>, but these remained minority views because it was difficult to specify in detail the relationships between these groups.
</p><p>In the 1990s, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics">molecular phylogenetic</a> analyses of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> sequences produced a coherent scheme showing arthropods as members of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superphylum" title="Superphylum">superphylum</a> labelled Ecdysozoa ("animals that moult"), which contained nematodes, priapulids and tardigrades but excluded annelids. This was backed up by studies of the anatomy and development of these animals, which showed that many of the features that supported the Articulata hypothesis showed significant differences between annelids and the earliest Panarthropods in their details, and some were hardly present at all in arthropods. This hypothesis groups annelids with molluscs and <a href="/wiki/Brachiopod" title="Brachiopod">brachiopods</a> in another superphylum, <a href="/wiki/Lophotrochozoa" title="Lophotrochozoa">Lophotrochozoa</a>.
</p><p>If the Ecdysozoa hypothesis is correct, then segmentation of arthropods and annelids either has evolved <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergently</a> or has been inherited from a much older ancestor and subsequently lost in several other lineages, such as the non-arthropod members of the Ecdysozoa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TelfordBourlatEtAl2008EvolutionOfEcdysozoa_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-TelfordBourlatEtAl2008EvolutionOfEcdysozoa-99">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogeny_of_stem-group_arthropods">Phylogeny of stem-group arthropods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Phylogeny of stem-group arthropods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Modern interpretations of the basal, extinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stem-group" title="Stem-group">stem-group</a> of Arthropoda recognised the following groups, from most basal to most crownward:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ortega-Hernández_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ortega-Hernández-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edgecombe2020_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edgecombe2020-102">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Lobopodia#Siberion_and_similar_taxa" title="Lobopodia">"Giant" or "Siberiid Lobopodians"</a>, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Jianshanopodia" title="Jianshanopodia">Jianshanopodia</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Siberion" title="Siberion">Siberion</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Megadictyon" title="Megadictyon">Megadictyon</a></i>, are the most basal <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_grade" title="Evolutionary grade">grade</a> in the total-group Arthropoda.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Lobopodia#Gilled_lobopodians" title="Lobopodia">"Gilled Lobopodians"</a>, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Kerygmachela" title="Kerygmachela">Kerygmachela</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Pambdelurion" title="Pambdelurion">Pambdelurion</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Opabinia" title="Opabinia">Opabinia</a></i>, are the second most basal grade.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Radiodonta" title="Radiodonta">Radiodonta</a>, which traditionally known as anomalocaridids come in third position, and are thought to be <a href="/wiki/Monophyly" title="Monophyly">monophyletic</a>.</li>
<li>A possible "upper stem-group" assemblage of more uncertain position<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edgecombe2020_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Edgecombe2020-102">[99]</a></sup> but contained within <a href="/wiki/Deuteropoda" title="Deuteropoda">Deuteropoda</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ortega-Hernández_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ortega-Hernández-1">[1]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Fuxianhuiida" title="Fuxianhuiida">Fuxianhuiida</a>, <a href="/wiki/Megacheira" title="Megacheira">Megacheira</a>, and multiple "bivalved forms" including <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Isoxyida&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Isoxyida (page does not exist)">Isoxyida</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Isoxys" title="Isoxys">Isoxys</a></i> and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Surusicaris&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Surusicaris (page does not exist)">Surusicaris</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Hymenocarina" title="Hymenocarina">Hymenocarina</a>.</li></ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Deuteropoda" title="Deuteropoda">Deuteropoda</a> is a recently established clade uniting the crown-group (living) arthropods with these possible "upper stem-group" fossils taxa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ortega-Hernández_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ortega-Hernández-1">[1]</a></sup> The clade is defined by important changes to the structure of the head region such as the appearance of a differentiated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deutocerebrum" title="Deutocerebrum">deutocerebral</a> appendage pair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ortega-Hernández_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ortega-Hernández-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, recent analyses since late 2010s also show that these "upper stem-groups" might be inside the crown-group:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edgecombe2020_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-Edgecombe2020-102">[99]</a></sup> isoxyids might nested with the crown-group itself,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZengH2020_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZengH2020-103">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OFlynn2022_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-OFlynn2022-104">[101]</a></sup> Megacheira have been recovered as more closely related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chelicerates" title="Chelicerates">Chelicerates</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZengH2020_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-ZengH2020-103">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OFlynn2022_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-OFlynn2022-104">[101]</a></sup> some bivalved forms such as Hymenocarina are consistently shown to be <a href="/wiki/Mandibulata" title="Mandibulata">mandibulates</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edgecombe2020_102-3"><a href="#cite_note-Edgecombe2020-102">[99]</a></sup> and similarly <a href="/wiki/Fuxianhuiida" title="Fuxianhuiida">Fuxianhuiida</a> might also be mandibulates as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AriaC2017_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-AriaC2017-105">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>The following cladogram shows the probable relationships between crown-group Arthropoda and stem-group Arthropoda according to O’Flynn et al. 2022, including two new fossils found to be the most early branches of <a href="/wiki/Deuteropoda" title="Deuteropoda">Deuteropoda</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZengH2020_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-ZengH2020-103">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OFlynn2022_104-2"><a href="#cite_note-OFlynn2022-104">[101]</a></sup> (the "upper stem-groups" in previous studies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ortega-Hernández_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-Ortega-Hernández-1">[1]</a></sup> are marked in asterisk, living groups are marked in bold):
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r984819063" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>

<p>Note that the subphylum <a href="/wiki/Artiopoda" title="Artiopoda">Artiopoda</a>, containing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trilobites" title="Trilobites">trilobites</a>, is closer to <a href="/wiki/Mandibulata" title="Mandibulata">mandibulates</a> than to <a href="/wiki/Chelicerata" title="Chelicerata">chelicerates</a> in the cladogram above,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZengH2020_103-3"><a href="#cite_note-ZengH2020-103">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OFlynn2022_104-3"><a href="#cite_note-OFlynn2022-104">[101]</a></sup> but older analyses place them as the sister group of <a href="/wiki/Chelicerata" title="Chelicerata">chelicerates</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edgecombe2020_102-4"><a href="#cite_note-Edgecombe2020-102">[99]</a></sup> united under the clade <a href="/wiki/Arachnomorpha" title="Arachnomorpha">Arachnomorpha</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogeny_of_living_arthropods">Phylogeny of living arthropods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Phylogeny of living arthropods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r984819063" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_arthropod_orders" title="List of arthropod orders">List of arthropod orders</a></div>
<p>The phylum Arthropoda is typically <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_classification" title="Scientific classification">subdivided</a> into four <a href="/wiki/Subphylum" title="Subphylum">subphyla</a>, of which one is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extinct" title="Extinct">extinct</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li><b><a href="/wiki/Artiopoda" title="Artiopoda">Artiopods</a></b> are an extinct group of formerly numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_animal" title="Marine animal">marine animals</a> that disappeared in the <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a>, though they were in decline prior to this killing blow, having been reduced to one order in the <a href="/wiki/Late_Devonian_extinction" title="Late Devonian extinction">Late Devonian extinction</a>. They contain groups such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trilobita" title="Trilobita">trilobites</a>.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Chelicerata" title="Chelicerata">Chelicerates</a></b> comprise the marine <a href="/wiki/Sea_spider" title="Sea spider">sea spiders</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe_crab" title="Horseshoe crab">horseshoe crabs</a>, along with the terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Arachnid" title="Arachnid">arachnids</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harvestmen" title="Harvestmen">harvestmen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">spiders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scorpion" title="Scorpion">scorpions</a> and related organisms characterized by the presence of <a href="/wiki/Chelicerae" title="Chelicerae">chelicerae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Appendage" title="Appendage">appendages</a> just above/in front of the <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_mouthparts" title="Arthropod mouthparts">mouthparts</a>. Chelicerae appear in scorpions and horseshoe crabs as tiny <a href="/wiki/Claw" title="Claw">claws</a> that they use in feeding, but those of spiders have developed as <a href="/wiki/Fang" title="Fang">fangs</a> that inject venom.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Myriapoda" title="Myriapoda">Myriapods</a></b> comprise <a href="/wiki/Millipede" title="Millipede">millipedes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Centipede" title="Centipede">centipedes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pauropod" title="Pauropod">pauropods</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symphylan" title="Symphylan">symphylans</a>, characterized by having numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Body_segment" title="Body segment">body segments</a> each of which bearing one or two pairs of legs (or in a few cases being legless). All members are exclusively terrestrial.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Pancrustacea" title="Pancrustacea">Pancrustaceans</a></b> comprise <a href="/wiki/Ostracod" title="Ostracod">ostracods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barnacle" title="Barnacle">barnacles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copepod" title="Copepod">copepods</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malacostracan" title="Malacostracan">malacostracans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cephalocaridan" title="Cephalocaridan">cephalocaridans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Branchiopod" title="Branchiopod">branchiopods</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Remipedes" title="Remipedes">remipedes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hexapoda" title="Hexapoda">hexapods</a>. Most groups are primarily <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_animal" title="Aquatic animal">aquatic</a> (two notable exceptions being <a href="/wiki/Woodlouse" title="Woodlouse">woodlice</a> and hexapods, which are both purely <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_animal" title="Terrestrial animal">terrestrial</a>) and are characterized by having <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_leg#Biramous_and_uniramous" title="Arthropod leg">biramous</a> appendages. The most abundant group of pancrustaceans are the terrestrial hexapods, which comprise <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diplura" title="Diplura">diplurans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Springtails" title="Springtails">springtails</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Protura" title="Protura">proturans</a>, with six <a href="/wiki/Thorax_(arthropod_anatomy)" title="Thorax (arthropod anatomy)">thoracic</a> legs.</li></ol>
<p>Aside from these major groups, a number of fossil forms, mostly from the early <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">Cambrian</a> period, are difficult to place taxonomically, either from lack of obvious affinity to any of the main groups or from clear affinity to several of them. <i><a href="/wiki/Marrella" title="Marrella">Marrella</a></i> was the first one to be recognized as significantly different from the well-known groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whittington1971Marrella_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Whittington1971Marrella-30">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogeny</a> of the major extant arthropod groups has been an area of considerable interest and dispute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[107]</a></sup> Recent studies strongly suggest that Crustacea, as traditionally defined, is <a href="/wiki/Paraphyly" title="Paraphyly">paraphyletic</a>, with Hexapoda having evolved from within it,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Regi10_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Regi10-111">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vonJennWillDell11_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-vonJennWillDell11-112">[109]</a></sup> so that Crustacea and Hexapoda form a clade, <a href="/wiki/Pancrustacea" title="Pancrustacea">Pancrustacea</a>. The position of <a href="/wiki/Myriapoda" title="Myriapoda">Myriapoda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chelicerata" title="Chelicerata">Chelicerata</a> and Pancrustacea remains unclear as of April 2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>. In some studies, Myriapoda is grouped with Chelicerata (forming <a href="/wiki/Myriochelata" title="Myriochelata">Myriochelata</a>);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hassanin_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hassanin-113">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[111]</a></sup> in other studies, Myriapoda is grouped with Pancrustacea (forming <a href="/wiki/Mandibulata" title="Mandibulata">Mandibulata</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Regi10_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Regi10-111">[108]</a></sup> or Myriapoda may be sister to Chelicerata plus Pancrustacea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vonJennWillDell11_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-vonJennWillDell11-112">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="float:left; max-width:100%; font-size:85%; border:solid 1px silver; padding:2px; margin:2px;"><div style="border:solid 1px silver; padding:5px;">

<div style="position:absolute;top:10.72em;color:black;white-space:nowrap">traditional <b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crustacea" title="Crustacea">Crustacea</a></b></div></div>

Phylogenetic relationships of the major extant arthropod groups according to Regier et al. (2010);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Regi10_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-Regi10-111">[108]</a></sup> traditional subphyla in bold</div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>The placement of the extinct <a href="/wiki/Trilobite" title="Trilobite">trilobites</a> is also a frequent subject of dispute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenner2006ChallengingReceivedWisdoms_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jenner2006ChallengingReceivedWisdoms-115">[112]</a></sup> One of the newer hypotheses is that the chelicerae have originated from the same pair of appendages that evolved into antennae in the ancestors of <a href="/wiki/Mandibulata" title="Mandibulata">Mandibulata</a>, which would place trilobites, which had antennae, closer to Mandibulata than Chelicerata.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the <a href="/wiki/International_Code_of_Zoological_Nomenclature" title="International Code of Zoological Nomenclature">International Code of Zoological Nomenclature</a> recognises no priority above the rank of family, many of the higher-level groups can be referred to by a variety of different names.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[114]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="self-published source? (May 2018)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>

<td><b><a href="/wiki/Chelicerata" title="Chelicerata">Chelicerata</a></b></td>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pycnogonida" title="Pycnogonida">Pycnogonida</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Xiphosura" title="Xiphosura">Xiphosura</a><br/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arachnida" title="Arachnida">Arachnida</a></td>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_Spider" title="Sea Spider">Sea Spiders</a><br/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horseshoe_Crab" title="Horseshoe Crab">Horseshoe Crabs</a><br/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harvestmen" title="Harvestmen">Harvestmen</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solifuge" title="Solifuge">Solifuges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">Mites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scorpion" title="Scorpion">Scorpions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">Spiders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tick" title="Tick">Ticks</a> etc.</td>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interaction_with_humans">Interaction with humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthropod&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Interaction with humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Insect_food_stall.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Insect_food_stall.JPG/220px-Insect_food_stall.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Insect_food_stall.JPG/330px-Insect_food_stall.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Insect_food_stall.JPG/440px-Insect_food_stall.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Insect_food_stall.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Insects and scorpions on sale in a food stall in <a href="/wiki/Bangkok" title="Bangkok">Bangkok</a>, Thailand</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Entomophagy" title="Entomophagy">Entomophagy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pollinator_decline" title="Pollinator decline">Pollinator decline</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">Crustaceans</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Crab" title="Crab">crabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lobster" title="Lobster">lobsters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crayfish" title="Crayfish">crayfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimp</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Prawn" title="Prawn">prawns</a> have long been part of human cuisine, and are now raised commercially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[115]</a></sup> Insects and their grubs are at least as nutritious as meat, and are eaten both raw and cooked in many cultures, though not most European, Hindu, and Islamic cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[117]</a></sup> Cooked <a href="/wiki/Tarantula" title="Tarantula">tarantulas</a> are considered a delicacy in <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[120]</a></sup> and by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piaroa" title="Piaroa"> Piaroa Indians</a> of southern <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>, after the highly irritant hairs – the spider's main defense system – are removed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[121]</a></sup> Humans also <a href="/wiki/Entomophagy#Unintentional_ingestion" title="Entomophagy">unintentionally eat</a> arthropods in other foods,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[122]</a></sup> and food safety regulations lay down acceptable contamination levels for different kinds of food material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[Note 4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[Note 5]</a></sup> The intentional cultivation of arthropods and other small animals for human food, referred to as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minilivestock" title="Minilivestock">minilivestock</a>, is now emerging in <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">animal husbandry</a> as an ecologically sound concept.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[126]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Commercial_butterfly_breeding" title="Commercial butterfly breeding">Commercial butterfly breeding</a> provides Lepidoptera stock to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Butterfly_house_(conservatory)" title="Butterfly house (conservatory)">butterfly conservatories</a>, educational exhibits, schools, research facilities, and cultural events.
</p><p>However, the greatest contribution of arthropods to human food supply is by <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollination</a>: a 2008 study examined the 100 crops that FAO lists as grown for food, and estimated pollination's economic value as €153 billion, or 9.5 per cent of the value of world agricultural production used for human food in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[127]</a></sup> Besides pollinating, <a href="/wiki/Bee" title="Bee">bees</a> produce <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a>, which is the basis of a rapidly growing industry and international trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>The red dye <a href="/wiki/Cochineal" title="Cochineal">cochineal</a>, produced from a Central American species of insect, was economically important to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztecs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Mayans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[129]</a></sup> While the region was under <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spanish</a> control, it became <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>'s second most-lucrative export,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[130]</a></sup> and is now regaining some of the ground it lost to synthetic competitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[131]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shellac" title="Shellac">Shellac</a>, a resin secreted by a species of insect native to southern Asia, was historically used  in great quantities for many applications in which it has mostly been replaced by synthetic resins, but it is still used in woodworking and as a food additive. The blood of horseshoe crabs contains a clotting agent, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Limulus_Amebocyte_Lysate" title="Limulus Amebocyte Lysate">Limulus Amebocyte Lysate</a>, which is now used to test that antibiotics and kidney machines are free of dangerous <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, and to detect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinal_meningitis" title="Spinal meningitis">spinal meningitis</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[132]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Forensic_entomology" title="Forensic entomology">Forensic entomology</a> uses evidence provided by arthropods to establish the time and sometimes the place of death of a human, and in some cases the cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[133]</a></sup> Recently insects have also gained attention as potential sources of drugs and other medicinal substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>The relative simplicity of the arthropods' body plan, allowing them to move on a variety of surfaces both on land and in water, have made them useful as models for <a href="/wiki/Robot" title="Robot">robotics</a>. The redundancy provided by segments allows arthropods and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biomimesis" title="Biomimesis">biomimetic</a> robots to move normally even with damaged or lost appendages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; margin-left:5px">
